Power System Dispatch
Combining Stochastic
Programming and Robust
Optimization
Ignacio Blanco Iñigo
Kongens Lyngby 2015
==PAGE:2==
Technical University of Denmark
Department of Applied Mathematics and Computer Science
Richard Petersens Plads, building 324,
2800 Kongens Lyngby, Denmark
Phone +45 4525 3031
compute@compute.dtu.dk
www.compute.dtu.dk
==PAGE:3==
Summary
Electricity markets are formed by generators and consumers agents who sell and buy
electricity according to expectations. These markets conﬁde the amount of electricity
generated and consumed to deterministic predictions. Forecast electricity consumption
in the future is an essential aspect to calculate generation needs and grids distribu-
tion parameters. Similarly, it is of importance to forecast electricity consumption in a
mid-short term. Day to day and hour to hour adjustments are made based on the cir-
cumstances of the moment and these adjustments have an operation cost. Nowadays,
integration of renewable energy into the electrical system complicates the predictions
of the amount of energy produced by the generating agents. Hence, it is necessary to
introduce stochastic models to determine, more accurately, the amount of energy that
may be dispatched in the Day-Ahead market.
The aim of this thesis is to propose a new model in order to solve an economic dispatch
problem and compare the results obtained with a stochastic programming model and
a robust optimization model. This new model named Hybrid Economic Dispatch is
formed by the combination of the robust optimization and the stochastic program-
ming merged in the same model. The purpose is to explore new diﬀerent ways to solve
the economic dispatch problem. The concept of scenario partitions is introduced; the
k-means clustering method is used in order to cluster the scenarios within diﬀerent par-
titions. MATLAB is used to simulate these clusters. A Stochastic Economic Dispatch
and a Robust Economic Dispatch are developed in order compare the results obtained
in these two models with the results obtained by the Hybrid Economic Dispatch.
Benders decomposition technique is applied to provide a simpliﬁed solution for the
Hybrid Economic Dispatch. Speciﬁcally, two approaches are proposed, the ﬁrst one
is Benders decomposition with dual cut and the second one is Benders decomposition
with primal cut. All the models throughout this section are implemented in GAMS.
A two-node case system is solved for all the models and the results obtained are
==PAGE:4==
ii
used to describe the behavior of the models. In order to study the eﬀectiveness of the
diﬀerent models, two study cases are introduced. The ﬁrst case is a 14-node system
and the second case is a 72-node system. For the simulation of the ﬁrst model 100
wind power production scenarios are simulated, for the second case 50 scenarios.
The Hybrid Economic Dispatch provides an intermediate solution between the Stochas-
tic Economic Dispatch and the Robust Economic Dispatch. The two Benders decom-
position problem simpliﬁes the Hybrid Economic Dispatch, saving computational time
for cases with a large quantity of data such as the 72-node system. It is demonstrated
how the k-means method improves the solution obtained in the Hybrid Economic Dis-
patch for the 14-node system and 72-node system.
Keywords: Optimization, Stochastic Programming, Robust Optimization, Electricity
Markets, Economic Dispatch, Wind Power, Uncertainty, Forecasting, Decomposition
Techniques, Benders Decomposition, Partitions, K-means.

Preface
This thesis, credited as 35 ECTS, was prepared at DTU Compute - Department of
Applied Mathematics and Computer Science in fulﬁllment of the requirements for
acquiring the Msc. in Energy Engineering at Technical University of Madrid (UPM).
The duration of the project was 6 months, from the 10th of September 2014 to the
10th of March 2015.
The thesis has been carried out under the supervision of Professor Juan Miguel Morales
González, Doctor Marco Zugno from DTU Compute and the external supervision of
Professor Pablo Reina Peral from UPM.
Lyngby, 10-March-2015

==PAGE:21==
Chapter 1
Introduction
The continuous emissions of greenhouse gases pose a serious threat to the global envi-
ronment and it is speciﬁcally the reduction of CO2 emissions that is essential in order
to prevent the global warming. The ﬁrst step towards sustainability is to decrease the
usage of fossil fuels and instead implement renewable energy sources. Therefore, the
energy extracted from non-fossil sources needs to be aﬀordable, reliable, safe, ﬂexible
in usage and limitless (Dell and Rand, 2001). This need for renewable energy solutions
has aﬀected the international political atmosphere to a great extent. Since 1997 the
European Union has been working towards a renewable energy supply equivalent to
12% of the total EU’s energy consumption by 2012 and an additional 8%, resulting in
20%, by year 2020. These common goals have encouraged the European countries and
today some are providing alternative technological solutions and policies in order to
integrate these renewable sources of energy. This continuous increment of renewable
energies face new challenges to be solved. The challenges are speciﬁcally great for
sources that are characterized by their unpredictable behavior, such as wind and solar
power that are aﬀected by meteorological factors.
In the electricity market, most of the relevant decisions that have to be made by the
market agents and the power system operators already involve a signiﬁcant level of
uncertainty. Therefore, the integration of renewable and unpredictable sources within
the market has proven to be a great challenge. In order to make the right decisions
it is therefore critical to achieve accurate models of natures behavior and the risk and
consequences of the uncertainty involved. Therefore, optimizing techniques that deal
with uncertainty of supply and demand are needed.
==PAGE:22==
2
Introduction
Stochastic programming is a method that provides an appropriate mathematical mod-
eling framework that characterizes the uncertainty and provides optimal decisions
(Conejo et al., 2010). These decisions have to be made one day in advance, before the
energy is delivered, in the day-ahead market. Using stochastic models it is possible to
determine the amount of energy that may be dispatched in the day-ahead market with
higher accuracy. The aim is to avoid the need of energy adjustments in the balancing
market, as the minimization of balancing actions in turn reduces the system costs.
The scope of this paper is to develop and propose a stochastic programming model
that will optimally adapt the market decisions, which are based on unknown elements.
As a ﬁrst step, such a model and its solution is ﬁrst obtained using robust optimization
and is later evaluated by comparing it to the optimal solution obtained by stochastic
programming.
Secondly, an alternative model that combines stochastic programming and robust
optimization will be analyzed. The solution obtained by their combination will be
evaluated and compared to the solutions of the robust optimization and stochastic
programming respectively.
1.1
Problem Statement
The aim of this work is to provide a new model that solves an economic dispatch
problem. It combines robust optimization and stochastic programming in order to ﬁnd
a solution that provides a diﬀerent alternative. The solutions of these three models are
evaluated in terms with the economic objective of minimizing the operation system
cost. The Benders decomposition technique is applied for this new hybrid method
between robust optimization and stochastic programming. Its aim is to simplify the
hybrid problem in order to make it more compatible. The results obtained from these
decomposition techniques are compared with the results obtained by the extensive
form of the hybrid problem.
1.2
Literature Review
Morales et al. (2013) provides a complete guideline of how renewable energies are
integrated into the electricity markets. This book, along with Conejo et al. (2010) are
the guidelines used in order to develop the diﬀerent economic dispatch models under
uncertainty. Benders decomposition is described numerous times in literature. Several
Benders decomposition problems have been examined, but this research focuses on
two case studies Shahidehpour and Fu (2005) and Leuthold (2009). The case studies
developed in Chapter 5 are the same cases used by Orduris (2014).
==PAGE:23==
1.3 Thesis Structure
3
1.3
Thesis Structure
The thesis is formed by 6 chapters. Chapter 1 contains the motivation for this work,
literature review and the problem statement. In Chapter 2, background information
of the electricity market is introduced. The day-ahead market, the reserve capacity
market and the balancing market are explained in detail. In order to get an in-depth
understanding of each market, a short market-clearing problem is introduced for each
of the diﬀerent markets. This structure allows the reader to get a full understanding
of how the energy dispatch problem is formulated. Chapter 3 includes the Stochastic
Economic Dispatch Model, the Robust Economic Dispatch Model and the Hybrid
Economic Dispatch Model. Chapter 4 presents the Benders decomposition method for
the Hybrid Economic Dispatch Model. Two diﬀerent problems are suggested. The
ﬁrst is Benders decomposition method with dual cut and the second one is Benders
decomposition method with primal cut. In Chapter 5, two diﬀerent cases of study
are presented in order to study the behavior of the diﬀerent models.
Finally the
conclusions and future work proposals are developed in Chapter 6.
==PAGE:24==
4
Introduction
==PAGE:25==
Chapter 2
Large-Scale Integration of
Wind Power into Electricity
Markets
The integration of wind power into electricity markets requires conquering the chal-
lenge of integrating regulation strategies. Complying with regulation is important as
wind turbines need to interact with the rest of the generation units in order to insure
the balance between supply and demand (Lund, 2005).
Uncertainties in wind forecasts force energy dispatch to be done in a non traditional
way, complexifying the operator’s work. This also limits the ability of wind power
plants to supply new loads. Several studies indicate that the capacity of a wind farm
is often below the nominal ability. However with high levels of integration, opera-
tional methods must be redesigned, since the wind farms can not be dispatched with
the same certainty as the rest of the production units in the system, as the wind power
production can ﬂuctuate by several hundred MW. The European strategy has been
successful regarding the management of this variability. This has mainly been based
on good predictive models, interconnections between neighbouring systems, operating
the system and taking decisions in short periods of time, technology improvements in
wind turbines, new policies on balancing markets and geographic dispersion.
==PAGE:26==
6
Large-Scale Integration of Wind Power into Electricity Markets
2.1
Electricity Market
In order to understand the decision making under uncertainty processes within the
electricity market, it is vital to understand the electricity market itself. In this follow-
ing section the key parts of the electricity market are introduced.
Electricity can be seen as a commodity which can be sold, bought and traded. The
system in which this is carried out is called the electricity market, a market which
consists of two main categories; short-term trading and long-term trading, which both
will be explained later in this section. The price for which the electricity is traded is
set by bids and oﬀers by some of the agents of this market. The main agents that take
part in this market process are producers and non-dispachable producers, retailers and
consumers (Nogales et al., 2002).
The Agents
According to Conejo et al. (2010), the producers are the ones responsible and in charge
of the electricity generation which they later on sell, via the market, to the retailers
and consumers. The non-dispachable producers are producers with that generate elec-
tricity from non-dispatchable sources such as wind and solar power. All market agents
have to deal with this unsteadiness and integrate renewable sources in the market.
The retailers are companies that participate directly in the electricity market where
they buy the electricity and later deliver it to their costumers (the consumers). It is
also possible for the producers to sell the electricity directly to the retailers through
bilateral contracts, something that will be explained further on. The producers and
the retailers share the same goal, to increase their proﬁt. The proﬁt of the producer
is generated by sales to the retailer, meaning that the proﬁt increases as the price of
the electricity increases. The proﬁt of the retailer on the other hand is increased for
lower electricity prices.
There are two diﬀerent types of consumers that take part in this market, the big
consumers and the small consumers. The big consumers are for instance industrial
facilities that consume very high levels of electricity. As the consumption of electricity
for these types of consumers usually is very high, they are able to participate directly
in the market by purchasing their own speciﬁc quantity, making them to one of the
agents of the electricity market. Unlike the big consumers, the small consumers do
not consume as high quantities of electricity and are therefore not able to participate
directly in the electricity market. Instead, the electricity is provided to these types of
consumers by the retailers. Such consumers are for instance domestic consumers.
Since all these diﬀerent types of market agents are interacting in one single market, this
market must in turn be operated by other independent agents. These agents are the
Market Operators (MO) and the Transmission System Operators (TSO). The market
operator is responsible for the economic management of the market, i.e the market
==PAGE:27==
2.1 Electricity Market
7
operator determines the prices and the quantities traded in the market. The system
operator is a non-proﬁt entity that ensures the technical management of the system.
The system operator is obliged to provide equal access to the grid to all consumers,
retailers and producers (Martínez-Crespo et al., 2007).
Market
Operated by:
Market Operator
System Operator
Retailers
Consumers
Producers
Non-dispatchable
Producers
Figure 2.1: Market Agents
As a majority of the electricity markets were liberalized a decade ago, they tend
to diﬀer from each other. In Europe for instance, diﬀerent electricity markets such as
Nord Pool/MIBEL, the market operator and transmission system operator are working
separately in order to ensure the lowest possible cost and availability of power to all
costumers. This diﬀers from the US model where there is just one Independent System
Operator (ISO) that carries out both functions (Gan et al., 2007) and (Oksanen et al.,
2009).
Long-term trading
As mentioned earlier, the electricity market is divided into two main trading categories,
long-term trading and short-term trading. Long-term trading is based on bilateral
contracts and future markets one year to one week before the energy is delivered.
These types of bilateral contracts are especially useful in countries and regions where
the generation activity is highly concentrated. By introduction of futures and bilateral
contracts, it is possible for diﬀerent agents to achieve a better allocate of the possible
risks (Herguera, 2000).
Short-term trading
Short-term trading involves mainly ﬁve diﬀerent markets: the day-ahead market, the
reserve market, the regulation market, the adjustment market and the balancing mar-
ket.
The day-ahead market occurs the day before that the electricity is delivered,
==PAGE:28==
8
Large-Scale Integration of Wind Power into Electricity Markets
Future Market
Operated by:
Market Operator
Short-term Market
Operated by:
Market Operator
System Operator
Retailers bids
Consumers bids
Producers oﬀers
Non-dispatchable
Producers oﬀers
Figure 2.2: Two diﬀerent markets
where the delivery date is denoted (d) and the day before delivery is denoted (d-1).
It is in the day-ahead market which the highest quantity of electricity is traded. This
means that in day (d-1) the producers and consumers submit bids to the market pool
on the quantity of electricity that will be supplied or consumed in diﬀerent periods of
time. These bids must be submitted before noon, which is when the day ahead market
closes (the day-ahead market is determined between 12 and 35 hours prior to delivery)
(Manual, 2011).
In order to secure the system operation and the actual energy delivery, the reserve
market and the regulation market occur. The reserve market is executed the day be-
fore the energy is delivered, i.e. at day (d-1). This market secures the system against
real time imbalances between the energy dispatched and the real time energy con-
sumed. It allows the system operator to deal with imbalances that may occur during
the real time market.
The regulation market provides the ﬂexibility that is needed in rider to increase
or decrease the production and consumption. In this way it constantly guarantees the
correct network operation. The regulation market is conducted by the system opera-
tor. It is carried out after the day-ahead market and the intra-days market sessions.
The market operator send the information to the system operator, who in his turn de-
termines if the information sent by the market operator is feasible and meets all safety
criteria. In case it does not meet all safety criteria, the system operator is obliged to
review new oﬀers in order to fulﬁll the criteria. The clearing price criteria in the regu-
lation market is the same as in the day-ahead market, it is always the highest activated
bid of each hour of operation that determines the price (Stoft, 2002) and (Weron, 2007).
The adjustment market has been established in order to guarantee an eﬃcient bal-
ancing operation of the power system against the renewable energy uncertainty. The
trading itself is allowed during the delivery day (d) and the trading procedure in the
adjustment market is divided into intra-day markets.
These intra-day markets are
carried out somewhere between the day-ahead market and the balancing market. Fur-
thermore, they are considered useful when it comes to integration of renewable energy
in the market, as forecasts are more accurate one hour before the energy delivering
rather that 24 hours ahead. This means that this market is avoiding the use of regu-
==PAGE:29==
2.2 Day Ahead Market
9
lation services in the balancing market and by that additional costs are spared.
The ﬁnal market of this introduction is the balancing market. It is operated at the
same time as the energy is being delivered. This market is carried out by the sys-
tem operator which is using the reserve previously dispached in the reserve market to
amend all the imbalances produced during a real time operation of the system. The
structure of this short-term trading is illustrated in Figure 2.3.
In this work, the regulation market and the adjustment market are excluded from
Figure 2.3: Sessions scheduled in short-term electricity markets. Source:(Manual, 2011)
further analysis. This is because the regulation market is based on optimal ﬂow the-
ory which is out of the scope of this project. The adjustment market and intra-days
markets are not thoroughly presented as their operating principles are the same as in
the day-ahead market.
2.2
Day Ahead Market
The aim of the day-ahead market is to deﬁne the market prices and the amounts
of electricity that the producers will generate and that the consumers will consume
during a speciﬁc time period. The price of the day-ahead market is determined in
the following way: the producers and consumers will announce theirs prices within an
auction. The producers submit 24 electricity blocks and their corresponding selling
prices, one oﬀer per hour. The exact same procedure is carried out from the consumers’
side, they will submit another 24 electricity blocks with their corresponding purchase
prices.
Producers are usually submitting their oﬀers at the minimum selling price
while consumers are submitting theirs consumption bids at the maximum buying prices
(Martínez-Crespo et al., 2007).
==PAGE:30==
10
Large-Scale Integration of Wind Power into Electricity Markets
Figure 2.4: Market-Clearing system price Source:(OMIE, 2015)
In Figure 2.4, an auction in the Iberian Electricity Market is illustrated. Usually, the
highest price bid of the demand is put by the retailers since they need to meet the sup-
ply demand and in this way are guaranteed to enter the market. On the other hand,
the lowest purchase bids come mostly from the wind power producers, solar power
and nuclear energy, these then rise as the Combined Heat and Power (CHP) systems
and coal power plants start presenting their bids. Finally, the highest purchase price
belongs to the old technologies such as fuel oil. From this illustration it is possible
to distinguish that the hydro power plants set the prices at the clearing value as this
kind of technology always is dispatching at its opportunity cost. The intersection of
the purchase bids and generation oﬀers will set the clearing price.
The day-ahead market is a crucial tool which is utilized when it comes to planning
the production of big producer units, especially nuclear power plants or coal power
plants in which some technical constraints do not allow an immediate respond. These
kind of units are too rigid and it becomes infeasible for them to produce without any
planning. The opposite situation in the day-ahead market are the renewable energy
producers. As this kind of technology involves a high grade of unpredictability and an
exact prediction for one day in advance is therefore diﬃcult, it is able to be integrated
in the day ahead market knowing that they will not be able to deliver the exact agreed
energy amount (Morales et al., 2013).

A two-nodes system is considered, see Figure 2.5, with three conventional generators
(G1,G2 and G3) oﬀering respectively production at 20,35 and 45 $MW −1h−1 with a

with a demand of 70 and 65MWh. The demand is fully inelastic so in case this load
is not supplied, the consumer will be compensated with 150$MW −1h−1, this is the
cost for shedding load. This small system is formed for just one transmission line with
capacity is 80 MW and its reactance is 0.15 pu. Node 1 is established as the reference
node for this two-node system case.

As the prediction of the wind power production is complicated for one day in ad-
vance, it is necessary that we add reserve capacities to the problem. This reserves
dispatch will allow to the operator dealing with any unpredictable change. In this
way, generators could redispatch diﬀerent quantities of electricity than the quantity
dispatched in the day-ahead market.
2.3
Reserve Market
In the reserve market, the generators are announcing bids to sell the ability to increase
the production if they are willing to do so (Grijalva et al., 2007). This provide ﬂexi-
bility power during the real time operation of the power system. This reserve becomes
necessary because there is always a possibility for the main components in a power
system to fail, such as an unpredictable behavior of the demand or the so-called black-
outs in the generation units. This quantity of energy that they are willing to produce
once they are requested to do so is called the reserve. In order to maximize proﬁts,
the producer determine the best combination of bids in order to produce energy and
provide a reserve.
Producers and consumer are both able to participate in this market by submitting
==PAGE:35==
2.3 Reserve Market
15
oﬀers to sell and bids to buy (Wang et al., 2003). In this way producers can sup-
ply downward reserve and upward reserve. A generation unit that is selling a certain
amount of its capacity as energy, cannot sell this quantity as upward reserve but it can
sell it as downward reserve. That is basically why the reserve market is an independent
auction within the same program as the day-ahead market.
This reserve market establish a mechanism for the power production units to reschedule
their production in real time and by doing so, increasing or decreasing the production
in order to maintain the balance between demand and supply. This is called secondary
frequency regulation and has to be available between 2 seconds and 15 minutes after
they are requested. As this is a regulation service, sometimes an amount of energy
produced in this market is much more ﬁnancially rewarded than the same amount in
a conventional energy dispatch.
Apart from the secondary frequency regulation, there is also a primary frequency
regulation. The primary frequency regulation is automatically produced for the gen-
eration units to compensate the imbalances. Since these primary regulators consist
of very large machines with a large mass rotating at high speed, they are able to
use their own inertia in order to compensate these imbalances for a few seconds until
the secondary regulation acts. This service has no retribution for being a mandatory
service.
2.3.1
Two-node Example for the Reserve Market
Energy and reserve should be cleared simultaneously in a co-optimization problem.
For this case, we will develop the two-node case of Figure 2.5 in Section 2.2.1 in order
to clarify this co-optimization problem between energy and reserve.
In this problem we will therefore add the upward reserve and the downward reserve.
This reserve’s capacity has an associated cost for every generation unit. The cost they
are willing to provide the upward reserve and downward reserve depends on how ﬂex-
ible a unit is in order to produce energy.
This data cost are illustrated in Table 2.1.
From the Table 2.1 we can distinguish diﬀerences between the energy oﬀer cost and
the reserve cost. For those units which have a higher production cost, the reserve cost
is lower than generation units which in turn are oﬀering energy for a lower cost. All
this is due to the technology of these units. An example, take for instance a nuclear
power plant that oﬀers electricity production for a lower price than a coal unit. The
nuclear power plants have less ﬂexibility to increase or reduce the production, unlike
the coal power plants. Hence, usually the reserve price increases since the production
cost decreases.
It is tough to predict the needed amount of reserve without having any kind of pre-
diction one day in advance, such as the wind conditions which directly aﬀect the wind
==PAGE:36==
16
Large-Scale Integration of Wind Power into Electricity Markets
power production, or any imbalance such us a blackout in one of the generation units.
In this paper, the method we have chosen to use, in order to predict the amount of
reserve that is needed, is to estimate the upward and reserve capacity equal the 50%
of the wind forecast, which is the same procedure as Morales et al. (2015).

2.4
Balancing Market
The system operation in real time is carried out in the balancing market and/or in
the real-time market. In this market the system operator (TSO) continuously ensures
the balance between the demand and the supply. This balancing market takes place
the same day the energy is produced, just before delivery, and is basically used to
price deviations in supply and demand from the day-ahead or in long-term contracts
(ENTOSE, 2015).
Having an eﬀective balance ensures the security of supply at the least cost while
reducing the need for buck-up generation. An important approach of the balancing is
the procurement of ancillary services. With ancillary services we refer to a determined
range of functions, which the TSO is contracting in order to guarantee the system
security.
These include black start capability (the ability to restart a grid after a
blackout), frequency response (to maintain system frequency with automatic and fast
responses), fast reserve (which provides additional energy when needed) and the pro-
==PAGE:38==
18
Large-Scale Integration of Wind Power into Electricity Markets
vision of reactive power and various other services. This capability to access all these
services from a wide range of providers, including generators and demand response
(which involves customers changing their operating patterns to aid system balancing)
gives TSO:s ﬂexible options, which allows them to make eﬃcient decisions.
As the integration of the renewable energies in the electricity market follow the de-
mand, it becomes more diﬃcult when forecasting fails. Hence, especial mathematical
tools are needed in order to clear the system in a proper way. These tools allow dis-
patching all the available resources to meet the imbalances between the actual and the
scheduled load, generation and network congestion. It is clear that the TSO is address-
ing the real time market and it is selecting the least costly resources in order to meet
these imbalances and at the same time purchase replacements for ancillary services.
Furthermore, the TSO opens the market for all participants in order to take part in
a real-time competition (Song and Wang, 2003). All of these generators can submit
to the TSO with their upward and downward bids for the replacement of balancing
reserves, which we previously dispatched in Section 2.3. It becomes really important
as well for the bilateral contracts. In case of congestion, it becomes diﬃcult to ﬁnd a
way to change the contract. There are several methods for the both parties involved
in a contract to manage this situation, such as agree on which part that should be
curtailed in case of congestion. This detail is out of scope in this work.
Even though that there is no doubt that the TSO:s are doing their best, sometimes
imbalances occur. For these imbalances there are two possible solutions which depends
on if it is a positive imbalance or a negative imbalance. When a positive imbalance
occurs, and they normally do when wind power production excess the expectations,
the the easiest and cheapest thing is to spill all the wind power production in excess for
a short period of time until the TSO reacts by calling the downward reserve available.
If a negative imbalance occurs, some loads have to be shedding. The consumer will
then be not able to receive the energy that has been agreed on. In this situation the
consumer gets paid a high quantity as compensation for their losses.
2.4.1
Two-node Example for the Balancing Market
For this example we are going to apply the two nodes system from section 2.2.1 in
ﬁgure 2.5. The balancing market takes place in order to cover the imbalances in the
system. In this example we refer to the balancing market while assuming that it cov-
ers the diﬀerences between the wind power production dispatched in the day-ahead
market and the wind power production in real time. Furthermore we will assume that
there is a constant demand in every load of the system.
There are two diﬀerent cases that can occur: when the wind power production is
higher than the expected value and when the there is a generation deﬁcit in the wind
power production side.
Firstly, a case where there is a deﬁcit in wind power production will be formulated. In
==PAGE:39==
2.4 Balancing Market
19
this case, the power of the conventional units will be increased in order to cover the
demand. This is the same quantity of energy that we previously dispatched as a re-
serve in the reserve market. The producer sells this quantity of energy in the balancing
market at the same price it was sold for in the day-ahead market. In fact, this is very
interesting for producers, because they are receiving money not just from the reserve
dispatched but also from the energy that is redispatched in the balancing market. It
is therefore more proﬁtable for producers to be willing to dispatch the reserve.
We will use the same data as in the optimization problems from sections 2.2.1 and
2.3.1. The reserve, the deviation angle and the wind power dispatched will be con-
sidered as ﬁxed values, where these values are the optimal values obtained in the
co-optimization problem (2.17-2.30) in section 2.3.1. We will consider a low scenario
in order to deﬁne a negative deﬁcit and then investigate how the model behaves. In
this case a production of 12.6 MWh is considered. Then the formulation problem is

market is 45$MW −1h−1 · (−15.75)MWh = −708.75$.
In order to conclude, it can be stated that when the imbalances are negative, up-
ward regulation is needed and when we have positive imbalances realized, downward
regulation is needed. Since it is impossible to predict negative or positive imbalances,
variables in balancing markets depend on the occurring scenario. Hence, these balanc-
ing variables or second stage variables depend on the scenario.

==PAGE:44==

Large-Scale Integration of Wind Power into Electricity Markets
==PAGE:45==
Chapter 3
Economic Dispatch Under
Uncertainty
All the agents involved in the electricity market have to make decisions. For example,
producers and consumers have to submit their oﬀers or bids to the pool ignoring the
electricity price or the quantity of energy which is going to to be consumed. Nowadays,
because of the integration of renewable energy into the electricity markets, unknown
data abound even more within the decision-making problems pertaining to these mar-
kets. Since renewable energy sources have an unpredictable behavior, nobody is able
to know exactly how much quantity of energy these sources will produce. Hence it
becomes impossible to dispatch a precise amount of energy one day in advance Conejo
et al. (2010).
Decision-making problems can be tackled adequately through optimization problems.
In this optimization problems, the input data can be deterministic, i.e.
perfectly
known data. Then, their optimal solution is achieved solving one simple optimization
problem. But, actually, a high quantity of the input data are unknown, such as wind
power production. This is when forecasting becomes crucial. These input data which
depend on forecasts, complicate the optimization problem. Because of this data the
decision-making problem can be implemented in diﬀerent ways.
Forecasts become crucial to the integration of renewable sources within electricity
markets. Only hydro power reservoirs are more dispatchable than the rest of sources,
basically because of the nonstorability of other types of renewable energy resources. In
conclusion, except for the hydro power reservoirs, the rest of renewable sources, from
==PAGE:46==
26
Economic Dispatch Under Uncertainty
a mathematical point of view, share a high level of similarity (Morales et al., 2013).
In this chapter, three diﬀerent optimization problems are developed in order to achieve
three diﬀerent optimal solutions for integrating renewable energies into electricity mar-
ket problems. Firstly an stochastic optimization problem is proposed in order to obtain
a dispatch solution that minimize the expected system operating costs. Secondly an
approximation of a robust optimization problem is developed in this way obtaining
a dispatch solution that is optimal for the worst-case realization of the uncertainty.
Finally, an hybrid model between stochastic and robust optimization is created. This
optimization problem is the main motivation for this work.
3.1
Stochastic Economic Dispatch Problem
For this problem, a stochastic programming model is developed in order to solve the
economic dispatch problem introduced in Section 2.5 when uncertain parameters are
involved.
Stochastic programming is a mathematical framework that provides an optimal so-
lution by solving optimization problems in which some of the parameters involves
are unknown (Birge and Louveaux, 2011). Stochastic programming started to be used
when for many linear programs, the actual values of the coeﬃcients were not available.
Stochastic programming assume that these unknown parameters are random and their
probability distributions are known and independent of the decision variables (Urya-
sev and Pardalos, 2001). In this work stochastic optimization aims to minimize the
expected operation cost of the system in an energy dispatch taking into account un-
certain wind power production.
The decision maker has to make optimal decisions under uncertainty throughout a
decision horizon. In this horizon a number of stages should be considered. Each stage
represents a point in time where decisions are made. According to the number of stages
it can be distinguished between two-stage and multistage decision making problems 1.
In this work the decisions are made in two stages. Decisions are made one day before
the energy delivering, in the day-ahead market. Figure 3.1 shows the scenario tree for
an economic dispatch problem.
1Multistage decision problems are out of scope of this work.
This kind of formulation
is really useful when the complexity of the decisions force the problem to be separated in
more than two stages. Normally multistage problems are used in long-term problems, when
the decision-maker has to plan throughout some years in advance since multistage decision
problems contains non-anticipativity constraints in later stages (Escudero et al., 2007)
==PAGE:47==
3.1 Stochastic Economic Dispatch Problem
27
Figure 3.1: Two-stage scenario tree - Source:(Escudero et al., 2009).
3.1.1
Stochastic Economic Dispatch Problem formulation
It should be clear for the reader what a recourse problem is. In all problems which
involve uncertainty, having a recourse becomes really useful. In these uncertain prob-
lems some of the decisions must be made before the uncertainty revealed. On the
other hand, another kind of decisions, the recourse decisions, can be made after the
values of the random variables are known. Hence, all these problems with recourse
decisions are called recourse problems. Considering a two-stage decision-making prob-
lem, according to Morales et al. (2013), the simplest recourse problem is the two-stage
stochastic programming problem, in which decisions are divided in to groups:
• Here and now or First stage decisions. These decisions have to be made
before the uncertain parameters realize.
• Wait and see or Second stage decisions. These decisions are made after the
uncertain parameters realize.
The ﬁrst stage variable vector named x is made before knowing the value of the stochas-
tic or uncertain parameter vector Ξ. The second stage decision vector y is made after
the stochastic parameter vector Ξ realizes. So the second stage decision vector de-
pends on the uncertain input parameter ϵ ∈Ξ. Then all second stage decisions can be
expressed as a function of the uncertain input y(ϵ).
It becomes necessary to characterize the stochastic parameter vector through scenar-
ios. According to Agustı et al. (2012), a scenario is a single realization of uncertainty.
Then uncertainty parameter vector can be represented as a function of the scenarios,
Ξ(w) being w = 1, ..., κ where w is the scenario index and κ is the number of scenar-
ios. Every possible realization of stochastic parameter vector Ξ(w) has an associate
probability π(w), where P
w∈Ωπw = 1.
==PAGE:48==
28
Economic Dispatch Under Uncertainty
Furthermore, the second stage decision vector y(ϵ) depends on the ﬁrst stage deci-
sion vector x which was previously made and depends as well on the realization of the
stochastic parameter vector Ξ(w). Because y(ϵ) is a function of the uncertain input
parameter ϵ and ϵ ∈Ξ(w), uncertain parameter ϵ can be expressed as a function of the
scenarios ϵ(w). Consequently y(ϵ) can be expressed as y(x, w). The decision making
process is represented in Figure 3.2.
Decision x is made
Stochastic process Ξ(w) occurs
Decision y(x, w) is made
Figure 3.2: Two-Stage Decision Making Process.

3.2
Robust Economic Dispatch Problem
In this section a robust approach to the economic dispatch problem under uncertainty
is adapted. It is important to highlight the concept of approach since this model is
a scenario-based approximation of a robust optimization problem.
This concept is
clariﬁed throughout the section.
Robust Optimization is a ﬁeld of optimization theory. This kind of optimization al-
lows to handle optimization problems with uncertainty in data in order to provide the
optimal solution. Robust Optimization is used to model situations where the range of
the uncertainty is known but not necessarily the distribution (Werner and Schöttle,
2010).
Typically, some inputs take an uncertain value between a ﬁxed minimum and a ﬁxed
maximum. Solutions will be feasible for all the constraints when the inputs assume
values within the uncertainty ranges. The robustness of the decisions will be qualiﬁed
according to the best performance among all the possible realizations of the parame-
ters values. In this case the best solution for a Robust Optimization problem is the
optimal solution for the worst-case scenario.
==PAGE:54==
34
Economic Dispatch Under Uncertainty
Table 3.4: System Operating Costs in the two-node example for the Stochastic Economic
Dispatch.
Cost [$]
Energy dispatched
40 · 20 + 44.6 · 35 + 18.9 · 45
=
3211.5
Reserve dispatched
6 · 18.9
113.4
Balancing actions
0.25(18.9 · 45 + 6.3 · 45 −6.3 · 45 −18.9 · 45)
0
Total
3324.9
Once the model reaches the optimal solution for the worst-case scenario, it is pro-
tected from the risk of uncertainty, providing an optimal solution which includes the
worst forecast of the uncertain parameters, In our case, the worst scenario of wind
power production (Bertsimas et al., 2011).
The most important diﬀerence between Stochastic Optimization (SO) and Robust
Optimization (RO) is that in SO the uncertain numerical data are assumed to be ran-
dom and these random data are modeled using a complex probability distribution.
Sometimes SO can be controversial because some parameters can not respond in a
probabilistic way. The SO approach is less conservative than the RO worst-case sce-
nario. Everything depends on how willing the decision-maker is to accept probabilistic
guarantees or if, on the contrary, it is preferred not to assume the risk and so accept
the reliance given by the RO worst-case.
Another diﬀerence is that, while with RO the best solution is obtained for the worst-
case scenario, with SO diﬀerent solutions are obtained for diﬀerent scenarios. These
scenarios can oscillate between very optimistic and very pessimistic scenarios. It de-
pends on the risk that we are willing to assume. If someone does not want to accept
risk, then this person will bet for a pessimistic scenario. On the other hand if someone
does not care about risk, then this person will bet for one of the optimistic scenarios
(Ben-Tal et al., 2009).

The problem presented above (3.71-3.77) is a three-level (min-max-min) optimization
problem, which follows the next sequence: First the upper-level problem aims to min-
imize the cost of the ﬁrst stage decisions vector x ∈X which includes the energy
dispatch in the day-ahead market, reserve capacity and planned voltage angles.
The mid-level problem selects the most adverse realization ϵ within the uncertainty
set Ξ. This most adverse realization is the worst-case scenario for the wind power
production.
The lower-level problem minimize the cost of the second stage decision vector which
includes the redispatched energy, load shedding, wind power spillage and the actual
voltage angles.
The solution of the worst case realization of uncertainty ϵ is one of the vertices of
the polytope formed by Ξ. The set of vertices of the polytope formed by Ξ is ﬁnite.
Since the number of vertices is ﬁnite, a solution approach based on the enumeration
of these vertices is possible (Zugno and Conejo, 2013).
This three-level problem, because of its structure is not computationally manageable.
To make it treatable an auxiliary variable β is introduced. This auxiliary variable
represents the worst-case recourse cost W[Q(y(ϵ))], which is the optimal value of the
inner max-min problem in (3.71) (Morales et al., 2013). Thus, problem (3.71-3.77) can
be reformulated as follows:
minimize
x,yυ,β
cT x + β
(3.78)
s.t. β ≥qT

Where υ is the index of the vertices of the the polytope formed by the uncertainty set
Ξ, being ΥΞ the complete set of vertices. Is here where the robust approach proposed
in this work diﬀers from the theory explained so far. In this work, no vertices are
considered. A ﬁnite set of scenarios is used instead of the complete set of vertices ΥΞ
formed by the polytope Ξ. As it was explained at the beginning of this section, this
approach is an approximation of the pure robust problem since the vertices of ΥΞ may
not be included in the selected set of scenarios. The auxiliary variable β is used to
approximate problem (3.71-3.77) as follows:

3.3
Hybrid Economic Dispatch Problem
In this section a new approach to solve the economic dispatch problem under uncer-
tainty is proposed. This solution approach is the main motivation for this work. As the
title of this Master Thesis describes, robust programming and stochastic optimization
will be merged in the same model. We named this model, Hybrid Economic Dispatch.
The complexity of the problem requires the development of a new methodology and a
new design of the model. This new methodology consists of making partitions in the
whole set of scenarios. The objective of these partitions is to split the set of scenarios
into some groups, and take the worst-case scenario from each group merging them in
the objective function in order to optimize the solution.
3.3.1
Scenario Partition
Consider the set of scenarios Ω= {1, ..., w, ..., κ}. This set of scenarios is divided into
diﬀerent groups of sets. These groups of sets are called partitions and they are formed
by scenarios from the set Ω. These partitions belong to the set P = {1, ..., p}, being
p the number of partitions. The index to denote the partitions is wp : ∀wp ∈P. One
single partition is formed by a certain number of scenarios from the set Ω. Hence, the
complete set of partitions P is formed by the complete number of scenarios Ω. It can
be established that w ∈P. Figure 3.3 introduces the idea of the scenario partitions.
1
2
κ
w
wp = 1
wp = 2
wp = p
∀w ∈(1, 2, ..., κ)
∀wp ∈(1, 2, ...p)
Figure 3.3: Partitions of the set of scenarios Ω.
Partitioning leads to an important thought, this thought is under which criteria are
the scenarios divided into partitions. Having a large set of scenarios to be divided into
==PAGE:62==
42
Economic Dispatch Under Uncertainty
partitions, the criteria under which they are divided becomes an important issue since
the way they are divided signiﬁcantly aﬀects the optimization problem value.
In order to obtain the optimal solution using scenario partitions we use the k-means
algorithm.
The k-means is a clustering method which according to Hobbs and Ji
(1999) yields the best clustering eﬃciency in a probabilistic production cost modeling
compared to several other partitioning methods.
In essence, the k-means algorithm is a method used to automatically divide a data
set into k groups, by selecting k initial clusters centers and redeﬁnes this center trough
an iterative process. The process works by assigning every data to its closest cluster
center. The next step is to updated each cluster center to be the mean of their data
previously assigned. The algorithm converges when there is no more changes by as-
signing data to clusters (Wagstaﬀet al., 2001). For this work, one cluster deﬁnes one
partition within the complete set of partitions P.
Figure 3.4 shows how the k-means algorithm separates data into diﬀerent clusters
Figure 3.4: Representation of k-means performance - Source:(McCullock, 2009).
using the proximity between them. It can be seen in Figure 3.4 how this algorithm
deﬁnes centroids within clusters to express means.
The k-means clustering method is used in this work for clustering the wind power
production since it is a scenario-dependent parameter. This function can be easily
implemented in MATLAB deﬁning the number of partitions to be implemented and
adding all the wind power production scenarios. Then MATLAB automatically pro-
duces the clusters.
These clusters deﬁne partitions for the wind power production
scenarios. Hence, this mapping created, named Pwp establishes a connection between
each scenario and partition to which it belongs, (w, wp) ∈Pwp.
3.3.2
Hybrid Economic Dispatch Problem Formulation
The Hybrid Economic Dispatch Problem is developed in this section in order to clarify
how stochastic and robust models merge in this new model. The same nomenclature
as in Sections 3.1.1 and 3.2.1 is used.

The auxiliary variable β now depends on the partition index wp. This new optimiza-

behaves in the same way as optimization problem (3.86-3.93) since it aims to provide
the best solution for the worst-case scenario within the complete set of scenarios Ω.
Behavior diﬀerences realize when stochastic formulation from the stochastic optimiza-
tion problem (3.9-3.14) from Section 3.1.1 is applied. It is this step when stochastic
programming and robust optimization merge. To this end, we need ﬁrst to compute

This new formulation resembles the stochastic programming one in that it aims to
minimize the expected value of every possible realization of the auxiliary variable βwp.
The reformulated optimization problem (3.153-3.160) is, in fact, what we refer to as
the Hybrid Economic Dispatch Problem.
==PAGE:64==
44
Economic Dispatch Under Uncertainty
On the one hand, robust optimization is applied since the hybrid model between
robust and stochastic (3.153-3.160) considers the worst-case scenario from every par-
tition to provide the optimal solution. On the other hand, stochastic theory is applied
as well since the model minimize the expectation of every worst-case realization of the
balancing cost for the complete set of partitions P.

To conclude, the Hybrid Economic Dispatch optimizes the system energy and reserve
dispatch against the worst-case realization from every partition, that is, against sce-
narios w1 and w3 in this two-node system example. Those scenarios are the worst-case
scenario for wp
1 and wp
2 respectively. Hence, it can be stated that the Hybrid Economic
Dispatch Problem is fulﬁlling its purpose.
The extensive form of the Hybrid Economic Dispatch for the dispatch of a power
system over a set of T time periods and including ramping constraints, is presented in
the Appendix E in Section E.3.
3.4
Comparing Stochastic, Robust and Hybrid
Economic Dispatch Models
In this section a depth comparison between the three models developed in this chapter
will be carried out. The comparison will be based on the two-node system used in the
previous examples. A comparison between system operation costs will allow to explain
how the diﬀerent models behave and what are their diﬀerences. Table 3.11 provides the
optimal cost of the power system dispatch for every model according to diﬀerent sce-
nario realizations. This table highlights how each economic dispatch model performs
under three diﬀerent criteria, namely the expected value of the system operating cost,
the worst-case system operating cost and the expectations of this conditional cost on
the considered sets of partitions.
Table 3.11 shows how the Stochastic Economic Dispatch provides a solution that
==PAGE:69==
3.4 Comparing Stochastic, Robust and Hybrid Economic Dispatch Models49

performs the optimal result when the expected value of the system operating cost
occurs.
On the other hand if the worst-case realization of the scenario occurs the
solution provided by the Stochastic Economic Dispatch does not performs well. Ro-
bust Economic Dispatch provides a solution which performs the optimal result when
the worst-case system operating cost occurs, but provides the worst possible solution
when the expected value of the system operating cost occurs. The Hybrid Economic
Dispatch provides a solution that performs relatively well in terms of the expected and
the worst-case system operating cost.
The explanation for this behavior lies in the willingness of the decision-maker to as-
sume certain risk. On the one hand, if the decision-maker relies on forecasts and is
willing to assume a certain risk, then the Stochastic Economic Dispatch is the best
option. This model provides the optimal solution when the expected value of the sys-
tem operating cost occurs.
On the other hand if the operator is not willing to assume any risk, the Robust
Economic Dispatch is the best choice. Robust Economic Dispatch guarantee that the
worst realization of the uncertainties will occur. Therefore the solution provided by
this model is optimal when the worst-case scenario realizes. Then the robust solution
is more conservative than stochastic solution.
==PAGE:70==
50
Economic Dispatch Under Uncertainty
The Hybrid Hybrid Economic Dispatch provides a less conservative solution than the
Robust Economic Dispatch but more conservative than Stochastic Economic Dispatch.
Since the Hybrid Economic Dispatch assumes that the worst-case realization for ev-
ery partition will occur thus the solution obtained intermediates between the robust
solution and the stochastic one.
==PAGE:71==
Chapter 4
Solving the Hybrid
Economic Dispatch using
Benders Decomposition
In this chapter a Benders decomposition approach to Hybrid Economic Dispatch Model
presented in Section 3.3 is proposed. The economic dispatch problem is decomposed in
two easier problems and thus may provide a better computational behavior. Therefore
Benders decomposition may simplify the Hybrid Model.
According to Shahidehpour and Fu (2005) Benders decomposition is becoming a useful
tool to solve large scale optimization problems for power systems, since these problems
are combining technical restrictions and generation schedules in the same optimization
problem, therefore the size of these problems complicates the solving process. Conse-
quently Benders decomposition is applied in this work in order to study new ways to
make the problem easier to solve.
Section 4.1 presents a Benders decomposition approach using dual cuts, while a method
using primal cuts is described in Section 4.2. The same nomenclature as in previous
chapters is used throughout this section.
==PAGE:72==
52
Solving the Hybrid Economic Dispatch using Benders Decomposition
4.1
Benders decomposition with dual cuts for the
Hybrid Economic Dispatch Model
In this section a Benders decomposition approach to the Hybrid Economic Dispatch
from Section 3.3 is proposed.
According toConejo et al. (2006), two diﬀerent problem structures exist for which
decomposition techniques can be utilized, programs with complicating constraints
and programs with complicating variables.
The ﬁrst type can be solved applying
the Dantzig-Wolfe1decomposition algorithm, and the second can be solved applying
Benders decomposition (Leuthold, 2009).
In the economic dispatch problem, the ﬁrst stage variables (Pg,t, R+
g,t, R−
g,t, δ0
n,t) are
complicating variables. Complicating variable are deﬁned as those variables which,
when ﬁxed at particular values, render the remaining mathematical program rela-
tively easy to solve. Consequently the problem becomes substantially simpler (Conejo
et al., 2006).
The challenge is to deﬁne some ﬁxed values for the ﬁrst stage variables and a method
to adapt these values into an algorithm which provides an optimal solution. Benders
decomposition algorithm is able to manage this challenge.
4.1.1

Figure 4.1 shows how the ﬁrst stage variables obtained from the Master problem are

(4.25)
Where LB is the Lower Bound, UB is the Upper Bound, index ν ∈I is deﬁning
every iteration where I is the complete set of iterations. The problem converges when
|UB −LB| ≤ϵ being ϵ a tolerance value. Therefore the algorithm ends.
The complete Benders decomposition algorithm can be deﬁne as follows:
• Step 0: Initialization. First iteration ν = 1. The master problem is solved
without Benders cuts. The value of θ is assumed as 0.
• Step 1: Subproblems solution. Solve the diﬀerent subproblems in order to
obtain the Upper Bound.
==PAGE:75==
4.1 Benders decomposition with dual cuts for the Hybrid Economic
Dispatch Model
55
• Step 2: Master problem solution. Solve the Master problem adding all the
cuts from the previous iterations. The solution obtained is x(ν) and θ(ν) and a
new LB is obtained updating a new iteration (ν + 1) the algorithm goes to Step
1.
• Step 3: Convergence checking. If |UB −LB| ≤ϵ then the algorithm stops.
This process is shown in Figure 4.2.
Step 0: Initialization.
Step 1: Subproblems solution.
Step 2: Master problem solution.
Step 3: Convergence checking.
OPTIMAL SOLUTION
YES
NO
Figure 4.2: Flowchart of Benders Decomposition assuming feasibility.
The reader should notice that in the theory explained so far partitions have not been
considered. Since the main purpose of this section is to devise a decomposition method
for the Hybrid Economic Dispatch, partitions must be considered. Therefore the ﬁ-
nite set of scenarios Ωis separated into diﬀerent partitions. A new mapping between
scenarios and partitions (w, wp) ∈Pwp is indexed. The conceptual framework is the
same as in Figure 4.1, but in this case more more instances of the same algorithm are
implemented. The number of instances to be solved corresponds with the number of
partitions.
The algorithms are solved separately within an iterative process. When one of the
instance reaches the optimal solution, the model automatically goes to the following
instance for the next partition. This iterative process ﬁnishes when the instance for the
last partition is solved. After this iterative process all the partitions must be merged
into an Overall Problem. This Overall Problem merges all the cuts obtained from the
previous algorithms and ﬁxes the values of the dual variables calculated in the previous
processes. Consequently, an optimal solution for the complete set of partitions P is
obtained. Since it often occurs that the number of iterations (ν) is diﬀerent for each
partition, a new mapping has to be created in order to model how many iterations
correspond to every partition. This mapping is named (ν, wp) and is represented by
index Iwp.
Figure 4.3 represents the complete model by illustrating the process for which ev-

Table 3.10. We can conclude that Benders decomposition with dual cuts is an alterna-
tive solution method for the extensive formulation of the Hybrid Economic Dispatch
presented in Section 3.3.3.

4.2
Benders decomposition with primal cuts for
the Hybrid Economic Dispatch Model
In this section a new Benders decomposition scheme for the Hybrid Economic Dispatch
introduced in Section 3.3 is proposed. The main diﬀerence with the previous model
presented in Section 4.1 is related to how Benders cuts are formulated. In this case
cut coeﬃcient depend on primal rather than dual variables.
4.2.1

As it was explained in Section 4.1.1, Benders decomposition requires to deﬁne a Master
Problem and subproblems, in this case the subproblems are formulated as follows:

One subproblem (4.101-4.103) per scenario is created. The Benders decomposition
with primal cut for the Hybrid Economic Dispatch follows the same logic as the pre-
vious model developed in Section 4.1.1. Basically the Master Problem provides the
optimal values for the ﬁrst stage variables and these values are ﬁxed in the subprob-
lems. After the subproblems are solved, the one which is providing the highest value
of the objective function deﬁnes a mapping represented by index W. The scenario
corresponding to that subproblem is selected and included in the set W.
The next step is to deﬁne a Master Problem.
Now we refer the reader to Section
4.1.1 where each subproblem is adding a new cut to the Master Problem, therefore
the number of cuts is given by the number of scenarios. This statement is not valid
for this formulation of the problem. Since now just one Benders cut per iteration is
added to the Master Problem. This cut is given by the scenario which provides the
highest value among the subproblems objective functions. The number of cuts within
the Master Problem is determined by the set W. The Master Problem is formulated
as follows:
minimize
x,yw,β
cT x + β
(4.104)
s.t. β ≥qT
wyw
∀w ∈W
(4.105)
Ax = b
(4.106)
Dwx + Fwyw = gw
∀w ∈W
(4.107)
x ∈X
(4.108)
yw ∈Yw
∀w ∈W
(4.109)
The Master Problem’s solution is ﬁxed in the subproblems, subproblems are solved
and a new scenario is added to the set of scenarios W. The Master Problem is solved
again for the scenarios within the set W. Therefore, the algorithm is solved throughout
an iterative process. Figure 4.7 shows this iterative process.
The criteria used to stop the algorithm is the same as in Section 4.1.1 deﬁned by a
Lower Bound (4.110) and an Upper Bound(4.111)

ders process follows the same criteria as in Figure 4.2. The algorithm converges when

Figure 4.7: Flowchart of the interaction process between the Master Problem and the

|UB −LB| ≤ϵ.
In order to approach the Hybrid Economic Dispatch, the set of scenarios Ωis di-
vided into diﬀerent partitions (w, wp) ∈Pwp. One Benders algorithm is applied to one
partition. For the implementation developed in this work, when Benders algorithm
for one partition reaches the optimal solution, the Benders algorithm for the following
partition starts. This process continues until the algorithm for the last partition is
solved. The last step consists in deﬁning an Overall Problem in which all these par-
titions are merged. The primal cuts are deﬁned by the complete set of worst cases
scenarios W. If partitions are included, W is the union of the worst-case scenario sets
for each partitions, Wwp. This Overall Problem provides the solution for the complete
set of scenarios and partition (w, wp) ∈Pwp. Figure 4.8 shows schematically how this
process works.
The Overall Problem has the following structure:

==PAGE:92==

Solving the Hybrid Economic Dispatch using Benders Decomposition
a value of downward reserve equal to the minimum excess of wind power production.
The rest of wind power which cannot be redispatched is spilled at zero cost. Hence,
the objective functions of both subproblems take the same value. In cases like this
one, the model is implemented in order to selects the scenario with lower wind power
production, in this case w3.
Therefore, scenario w3 provides in both iterations the worst case realization for wp
2.

1 and in partition two
w3 is added to Wwp
2 . So ﬁnally the set is deﬁned as Wwp = {w1, w3} ∀wp ∈P.

composition approach with primal cuts are exactly the same results as for the Benders

==PAGE:95==
Chapter 5
Case Studies
In this section two study cases are developed. The aim of this chapter, is to show
the reader how the diﬀerent models processed throughout this work behave with large
number of data sets. So far the two-node system introduce in Section 2.2.1 has been
used to develop the models in a simple and understandable way. First case is a 14-node
system and second case is a 72-node system. These two cases will be represented for
a time period of 24 hours.
A large set of wind power production scenarios will be used. Detailed characteris-
tic of generators, buses, lines, nodes and wind producers are presented. These two
cases are taken from Orduris (2014).
5.1
Wind Power Production Scenarios
In this chapter we use wind power production scenarios from the Pinson et al. (2013)
study in which spatio-temporal dependencies of wind power generation were consid-
ered. This data provides a reliable input to the energy dispatch problem. The scenarios
were generated for 15 control areas and 43 lead times in western Denmark. However in
this work 24 lead times are implemented. Figure A.1 in Appendix A illustrates these
diﬀerent areas. 100 scenarios will be used for the 14-node system and 50 scenarios
for 72-node system. We consider that all scenarios have the same probability to be
realized.
==PAGE:96==
76
Case Studies
The complete set of 100 scenarios is represented in Figure A.2 in Appendix A. The
wind power production is given in p.u values normalized by the nominal capacity of
the wind farms (Orduris, 2014).
5.2
14-Node System
The 14-node power system is represented in Figure A.3 in Appendix A. All the in-
formation referred to this case is available in (UW, 2015). The reference node in the
system will be assigned as Node 1.
5.2.1
Input Data
All the input data needed are accurately developed throughout this section.

Table 5.1 provides the generation units data. These data deﬁnes node location, oper-
ation costs and technical restrictions of the generation units.

5.2.1.4
Wind Power Production Data
In this case study 100 scenarios are used and one control area is considered, control
area 2 shown in Figure A.1, allocated in Node 5. Figure A.5 in Appendix A shows the
wind power production for each scenario. The values of the wind power production
are given in p.u values. Therefore we consider a nominal capacity of 100MW.
The k-means clustering method is used to cluster the scenarios in diﬀerent partitions,
for this 14-node system we consider 10 partitions. The k-means clustering method
for 10 partitions is solved in MATLAB. The scenarios are clustered as a function of
the total production during the 24 hours. We refer the reader to the Appendix G in
Section G.3, where all the scenarios are implemented in MATLAB and the results ob-
tained after solving the k-means algorithm, determine in which partition the scenarios
are allocated.
The wind power dispatch is always assumed as the expected wind power production,

5.2.2
Results
In this section the results obtained in the 14-node system simulation for the diﬀerent
models are exposed.
Graphics and tables will be used throughout this section to
illustrate the results.
==PAGE:99==
5.2 14-Node System
79
5.2.2.1
Energy and Reserve Scheduled Results
Figure A.6 in Appendix A shows the optimal solution for the energy dispatch. The
ﬁgure shows which units are dispatched every hour for three diﬀerent models, the
Stochastic Economic Dispatch 3.1, The Robust Economic Dispatch 3.2 and the Hy-
brid Economic Dispatch 3.3. The three models behave in a similar way, most of the
energy dispatch comes from unit G3 and unit G4. During the early morning the wind
power production and these two units (G3 and G4) are capable covering the system
demand. During the peak hour, around 19:00, additional producer (G2) is needed to
cover the demand in the system. Unit G1 is never dispatched as the rest of unit power
producer G4,G3,G2 and the Wind farm are able to cover the demand throughout the
24 hours. The three models dispatch according the production costs, therefore accord-
ing to Table 5.1, G4 is the ﬁrst unit dispatched, followed by G3 which is the second
one and ﬁnishing with G2. Wind power is always dispatched as a ﬁrst option, since
the production cost is zero.
Figure A.7 in Appendix A illustrates the scheduled upward reserve for diﬀerent time
periods and the three diﬀerent models as in Figure A.6 in Appendix A. All the up-
ward reserve is provide by unit G4 since according to Table 5.1 provides the cheapest
cost for upward reserve. If we compare the diﬀerent models we can see that in the
Robust Economic Dispatch the higher amount of upward reserve is scheduled and the
lower amount of downward reserve scheduled is in the Stochastic Economic Dispatch.
This is basically because the Robust Economic Dispatch provides the solution for the
worst-case realization of the scenarios, therefore a higher quantity of upward reserve
is scheduled in order to cover this imbalances.
On the other hand the Stochastic
Economic Dispatch provides a lower amount of upward reserve since it provides the
optimal solution for the expected realization of the scenarios. Finally the Hybrid Eco-
nomic Dispatch model provides an intermediate solution between these two.
The energy and reserve dispatch results for the Benders decomposition models previ-
ously developed in Section 4.1 and Section 4.2 are shown in the Appendix A in Figure
A.8 and A.9 respectively. The amount of upward reserve in these two models approx-
imate to the Hybrid Economic Dispatch model from Figure A.7 in Appendix A.
No downward reserve is dispatched in the model. Since all units in the downward
reserve has an associate cost the models choose to spill wind power as there is no cost
associated.
5.2.2.2
Computational Results
Table 5.5 displays the total system cost associate to the Stochastic, Robust and Hybrid
Economic Dispatch models for the 14-node system. These results explain the behavior
of the models through diﬀerent realization of the scenarios. As was explained in Table
3.11, each economic dispatch model provides diﬀerent solution according to diﬀerent
criteria realization. The expected value of the system operating cost, the worst-case
==PAGE:100==
80

dispatch solution.

diﬀerences between elapsed time during the Hybrid Economic Dispatch simulation and
the elapsed time during the two Benders decomposition models simulation. This fact
is answered by the time needed for the Bender algorithm to compute the complete set
of scenarios and partitions. The 14-node system is not big enough to appreciate any
simpliﬁcation of the model using Benders decomposition. A model of this size can be
solve more quickly for the extensive form of the Hybrid Economic Dispatch.

5.2.2.3
Importance of clustering
This section aims to compare the results obtained from the expectation of the cost
conditional on the considered set of partitions when the partitions are clustered and
when they are not clustered through the k-means algorithm. Table 5.8 presents the
results for a randomly selection of the scenarios within the partitions for the Hybrid
Economic Dispatch problem and the Benders decomposition models.
We refer the reader to Table 5.5 and Table 5.6 where the k-means algorithm was
used to cluster the partitions. It can be appreciated that the solution showed in these
two tables for the Hybrid Economic Dispatch and the Benders decomposition models
provides a better economic dispatch than the solution displayed in Table 5.8.
==PAGE:103==
5.3 72-Node System
83
Therefore, for the 14-node case, when the partitions are clustered using the k-means
clustering method, the system provides a better dispatch solution for the same real-
ization of the scenarios.
5.3
72-Node System
The RTS-96 three area system (Lab., 2015) is illustrated in Figure A.10 in Appendix
A. The system is divided in three equivalent areas connected by 5 diﬀerent lines. The
reference node is considered as the ﬁrst node in the ﬁrst area (Node 101).
5.3.1
Input Data
As in Section 5.2 the input data necessary to solve the economic dispatch problem for
this 72 node data are accurately developed. For this new case all the input data tables
are located in the Appendix B. Exactly the same steps to deﬁne the data as in section
5.2 have been followed, power production data, demand data and transmission line
data. The price for shedding load is consider as 150$MW −1h−1.
For this study 50 scenarios are used and the 15 control areas from Figure A.1 are
considered. The nominal capacity considered for this case is 200MW. The scenarios
are clustered in 5 partitions through the k-means clustering method implemented in
MATLAB. Appendix G, Section G.3. The wind power dispatch is assumed as the
expected wind power production.
Since the areas are considered as equivalent, the tables exposed throughout this sec-
tion are providing the data for the 3 areas at once. If a data is speciﬁed for on speciﬁc
area number 1,2 or 3 respectively will be added in the hundreds of the speciﬁc data
number.
Table B.1 in Appendix B shows the generation units data for the complete set of gen-
erators within the system. The 3 areas are distinguished. Table B.2 in Appendix B
illustrates the demand hour by hour for one area of the system. The total demand of
the system is obtained summing up the total demand of the 3 areas. In this case the
demand is equivalent in the 3 areas. Table B.3 in Appendix B shows the node location
of the loads and the percentage of distribution in one area. The location of the loads
and the distribution among them is equivalent in the 3 areas. It means that in Table
B.3, L1 is connected to node 101, 201 and 203. The demand consumed per load and
per hour can be easily obtained from these two tables.
Table B.4 in Appendix B groups all the transmission line data for the 72-node system.
Origin and destination nodes, maximum transmission capacity and reluctance of every
line within the same area, considering equivalent areas. The diﬀerent 3 areas within
==PAGE:104==
84
Case Studies
the 72-node system are interconnected through ﬁve transmission lines exposed in Table
B.5 in Appendix B.
5.3.2
Results
The operation costs system results obtained from the simulation of the 72-nose system
are exposed in Table 5.9. The same realization as in Tables 5.5 and 5.6 is observed.
The Stochastic Economic Dispatch provides a solution that performs the optimal re-
sult when the expected value of the system operating cost occurs. Hybrid Economic
Dispatch on the other hand provides a solution that performs relatively well in terms
of the expected and the worst case system operating cost.
Diﬀerent behavior is observed between the Hybrid Economic Dispatch and the Ben-
ders decomposition with primal cut. In Tables 5.5 and 5.6 the total cost of the system
is equal between them, but the energy dispatch, reserve capacity dispatch and energy
redispatch costs are diﬀerent values for the two of them. In Table 5.11, the results
are exactly the same for the energy dispatch, reserve capacity dispatch and energy
redispatch costs. This issue is basically that the Benders decomposition with primal
cut needs more iterations to converge, since the dimension of this model makes it com-
plicated to solve. Every iteration is adding a new scenario to solve within the complete
set of worst-case scenarios. Then if a high quantity of scenarios are added the solution
obtained in the Benders decomposition with primal cut matches the solution obtained
by the Hybrid Economic Dispatch.
Table 5.10 shows the elapsed time during the simulations of the diﬀerent models
for the 72-node system. Compared Table 5.10 with Table 5.7 diﬀerences of time are
remarkable. The most important issue we should remark from this table is that Bender
decomposition methods save computational time compared to the Hybrid Economic
Dispatch Model. For systems with a high number of data to be processed such us this
case, the Benders decomposition make the problem more treatable. Another relevant
issue worth noting is the fact that in this case, the opposite to Table 5.7 occurs. For
this case Benders decomposition with dual cut needs less computational time to reach
the optimal solution than the Benders decomposition with primal cut. The complex-
ity of the 72-nodes system case forces the Benders decomposition with primal cut to
make more iterations, therefore the size of the problem becomes bigger increasing the
computational time.
Table5.11 illustrates the results obtained for the Hybrid Economic Dispatch and
Benders decomposition models using a randomly selection of the scenarios within the
partitions.
Compared with Table 5.9 in which scenarios are clustered through the
k-means algorithm, the results behave in the same way as in Section 5.2.2.3. There-
fore, as it was explained in Section 5.2.2.3 using the k-means function, all the models
perform providing a better dispatch solution for the same realization of the scenarios.
